<th scope="row">Religion</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarianism</a><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></td>

<tr>
<th scope="row">Signature</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg" class="image" title="John Quincy Adams's signature"><img alt="Cursive signature in ink" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg/128px-John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg.png" width="128" height="24" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg/192px-John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg/256px-John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="294" data-file-height="56" /></a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>John Quincy Adams</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/John_Quincy_Adams_pron.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/11px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/17px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/22px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="11" data-file-height="11" /></a><sup><span class="IPA" style="color:#00e;font:bold 80% sans-serif;padding:0 .1em"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_pron.ogg" title="File:John Quincy Adams pron.ogg">i</a></span></sup></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/?/ short 'i' in 'bid'">?</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="'z' in 'Zion'">z</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> July 11, 1767&#160;– February 23, 1848) was an American statesman who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">sixth</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> from 1825 to 1829. He also served as a diplomat, a Senator and member of the House of Representatives. He was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republican</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Anti-Masonic_Party" title="Anti-Masonic Party">Anti-Masonic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_parties_in_the_United_States" title="Political parties in the United States">parties</a>.</p>
<p>In his biography, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Flagg_Bemis" title="Samuel Flagg Bemis">Samuel Flagg Bemis</a> argues that Adams was able to: "gather together, formulate, and practice the fundamentals of American foreign-policy – self-determination, independence, noncolonization, nonintervention, nonentanglement in European politics, Freedom of the Seas, [and] freedom of commerce."<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>John Quincy Adams was the son of former President <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a>. As a diplomat, Adams played an important role in negotiating key treaties, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ghent" title="Treaty of Ghent">Treaty of Ghent</a>, which ended the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>. As <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, he <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">negotiated with Britain</a> over the United States' northern border with Canada, negotiated with Spain the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">annexation of Florida</a>, and drafted the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>. Historians agree he was one of the greatest diplomats and secretaries of state in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-Bemis_1949_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bemis_1949-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Herring.2C_George_p129_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herring.2C_George_p129-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As president he sought to <a href="/wiki/American_System_(economic_plan)" title="American System (economic plan)">modernize the American economy</a> and promote education. Adams enacted a part of his agenda and paid off much of the <a href="/wiki/National_debt" title="National debt" class="mw-redirect">national debt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> However he was stymied time and again by a Congress controlled by his enemies, and his lack of patronage networks helped politicians eager to undercut him. He lost his <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">1828 bid for re-election</a> to <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>.</p>
<p>Adams is best known as a diplomat who shaped America's foreign policy in line with his ardently nationalist commitment to <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">America's republican values</a>. More recently, he has been portrayed as the exemplar and moral leader in an era of modernization. During Adams' lifetime, technological innovations and new means of communication spread messages of religious revival, social reform, and party politics. Goods, money, and people traveled more rapidly and efficiently than ever before.<sup id="cite_ref-See_Howe_2007_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-See_Howe_2007-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Adams was elected as <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. Representative</a> from <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> after leaving office, serving for the last 17 years of his life with far greater acclaim than he had achieved as president. Animated by <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams_and_abolitionism" title="John Quincy Adams and abolitionism">his growing revulsion against slavery</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Lynn_H._Parsons_1999_p._232_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynn_H._Parsons_1999_p._232-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams became a leading opponent of the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a>. He predicted that if a civil war were to break out, the president could abolish slavery by using his <a href="/wiki/War_Powers_Clause" title="War Powers Clause">powers</a>. Adams also predicted the Union's dissolution over the slavery issue, but said that if the South became independent there would be a series of bloody slave revolts.<sup id="cite_ref-Parsons.2C_1999_p._162_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parsons.2C_1999_p._162-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life.2C_education.2C_and_early_career">Early life, education, and early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life, education, and early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>John Quincy Adams was born on July 11, 1767, to <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a> (née Smith) in a part of <a href="/wiki/Braintree,_Massachusetts" title="Braintree, Massachusetts">Braintree, Massachusetts</a> that is now <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Massachusetts" title="Quincy, Massachusetts">Quincy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> John Quincy Adams did not attend school, but was tutored by his cousin James Thax and his father's law clerk, Nathan Rice.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> He was named for his mother's maternal grandfather, <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy" title="John Quincy">Colonel John Quincy</a>, after whom <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Massachusetts" title="Quincy, Massachusetts">Quincy, Massachusetts</a>, is named.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> His namesake great-grandfather died only two days after he was born.</p>
<p>Adams first learned of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> from the letters his father wrote his mother from the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>. In 1779, Adams began a diary that he kept until just before he died in 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-Masshist.org_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masshist.org-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The massive fifty volumes are one of the most extensive collections of first-hand information from the period of the early republic and are widely cited by modern historians.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Much of Adams' youth was spent accompanying his father overseas. John Adams served as an <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_France" title="United States Ambassador to France">American envoy to France</a> from 1778 until 1779 and to the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> from 1780 until 1782, and the younger Adams accompanied his father on these diplomatic missions.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams acquired an education at institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Leiden_University" title="Leiden University">Leiden University</a>. He matriculated in Leiden January 10, 1781.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> For nearly three years, beginning at the age of 14, he accompanied <a href="/wiki/Francis_Dana" title="Francis Dana">Francis Dana</a> as a secretary on a mission to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, to obtain recognition of the new United States. He spent time in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and, in 1804, published a travel report of <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> During these years overseas, Adams <a href="/wiki/List_of_multilingual_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of multilingual Presidents of the United States" class="mw-redirect">became fluent in</a> French and Dutch and became familiar with German and other European languages. Adams, mainly through the influence of his father, had also excelled in classical studies and reached high fluency of Latin and Greek. Upon entering Harvard he had already translated Virgil, Horace, Plutarch, and Aristotle.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> He entered <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a> and was graduated in 1787 with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> degree, <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" title="Phi Beta Kappa" class="mw-redirect">Phi Beta Kappa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adams_House_(Harvard_University)" title="Adams House (Harvard University)">Adams House</a> at Harvard College is named in honor of Adams and his father. He later earned an <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts" title="Master of Arts">M.A.</a> from Harvard in 1790.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> He <a href="/wiki/Apprenticeship" title="Apprenticeship">apprenticed</a> as an attorney with <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Parsons" title="Theophilus Parsons">Theophilus Parsons</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newburyport,_Massachusetts" title="Newburyport, Massachusetts">Newburyport, Massachusetts</a>, from 1787 to 1789. He <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_practice_law" title="Admission to practice law">gained admittance to the bar</a> in 1791 and began <a href="/wiki/Practice_of_law" title="Practice of law">practicing law</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career_.281796.E2.80.931817.29">Early political career (1796–1817)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early political career (1796–1817)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Washington_administration">Washington administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Washington administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_-_Age_29.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/John_Quincy_Adams_-_Age_29.jpg/220px-John_Quincy_Adams_-_Age_29.jpg" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/John_Quincy_Adams_-_Age_29.jpg/330px-John_Quincy_Adams_-_Age_29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/John_Quincy_Adams_-_Age_29.jpg/440px-John_Quincy_Adams_-_Age_29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="638" data-file-height="754" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_-_Age_29.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Quincy Adams - Age 29</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams first won national recognition when he published a series of widely read articles supporting Washington's decision to keep America out of the growing hostilities surrounding the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. Soon after, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> appointed Adams <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_Netherlands" title="United States Ambassador to the Netherlands">minister to the Netherlands</a> (at the age of 26) in 1793. He did not want the position, preferring to maintain his quiet life of reading in Massachusetts, and probably would have rejected it if his father had not persuaded him to take it. On his way to the Netherlands, he was to deliver a set of documents to <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a>, who was negotiating the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a>. After spending some time with Jay, Adams wrote home to his father, in support of the emerging treaty because he thought America should stay out of European affairs. Historian <a href="/wiki/Paul_C._Nagel" title="Paul C. Nagel">Paul Nagel</a> has noted that this letter reached Washington, and that parts of it were used by Washington when drafting his <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address" title="George Washington's Farewell Address">farewell address</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>While going back and forth between The Hague and London, he met and proposed to his future wife, <a href="/wiki/Louisa_Adams" title="Louisa Adams">Louisa Catherine Johnson</a>. Though he wanted to return to private life at the end of his appointment, Washington appointed him <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Portugal" title="United States Ambassador to Portugal">minister to Portugal</a> in 1796, where he was soon appointed to the Berlin Legation. Though his talents were far greater than his desire to serve, he was finally convinced to remain in public service when he learned how highly Washington thought of his abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart,_1821-26.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg/170px-Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg/255px-Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg/340px-Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2517" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart,_1821-26.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a> <i>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Louisa_Adams" title="Louisa Adams">Louisa Catherine Johnson</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Washington called Adams "the most valuable of America's officials abroad," and Nagel believes that it was at this time that Adams first came to terms with a lifetime of public service.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He became a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1797.<sup id="cite_ref-AAAS_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-22"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> When the elder Adams became president, he appointed his son in 1797 as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Germany" title="United States Ambassador to Germany">Minister to Prussia</a> at Washington's urging. There Adams signed the renewal of the very liberal Prussian-American Treaty of Amity and Commerce after negotiations with Prussian Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Count_Karl-Wilhelm_Finck_von_Finckenstein" title="Count Karl-Wilhelm Finck von Finckenstein">Count Karl-Wilhelm Finck von Finckenstein</a>. He served at that post until 1801.</p>
<p>While serving abroad, in 1797 Adams also married Louisa Catherine Johnson, the daughter of a poor American merchant, in a ceremony at the church of <a href="/wiki/All_Hallows-by-the-Tower" title="All Hallows-by-the-Tower">All Hallows-by-the-Tower</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. Adams remains the only president to have married a First Lady born outside of the United States.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Massachusetts_politics">Massachusetts politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Massachusetts politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On his return to the United States, Adams was appointed a Commissioner of Monetary Affairs in Boston by a Federal District Judge; however, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> rescinded this appointment. He again tried his hand as an attorney, but shortly afterward entered politics. John Quincy Adams was elected a member of the Massachusetts State Senate in April 1802. In November 1802 he ran as a Federalist for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> and lost.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_General_Court" title="Massachusetts General Court">Massachusetts General Court</a> elected Adams as a Federalist to the U.S. Senate soon after, and he served from March 4, 1803, until 1808, when he broke with the Federalist Party. Adams, as a senator, had supported the Louisiana Purchase and Jefferson's Embargo Act, actions which made him very unpopular with Massachusetts Federalists. The Federalist-controlled Massachusetts Legislature chose a replacement for Adams on June 3, 1808, several months early. On June 8, Adams broke with the Federalists, resigned his Senate seat, and became a Republican.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-24"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvard_professor">Harvard professor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Harvard professor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While a member of the Senate, Adams also served as a professor of logic at <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Disowned by the Federalists and not fully accepted by the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Republicans</a>, Adams used his Boylston Professorship of Rhetoric and Oratory at Harvard as a new base.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams' devotion to classical rhetoric shaped his response to public issues. He remained inspired by classical rhetorical ideals long after the neo-classicalism and deferential politics of the founding generation had been eclipsed by the commercial ethos and mass democracy of the Jacksonian Era. Many of Adams' idiosyncratic positions were rooted in his abiding devotion to the Ciceronian ideal of the citizen-orator "speaking well" to promote the welfare of the polis.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams was influenced by the classical republican ideal of civic eloquence espoused by British philosopher <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams adapted these classical republican ideals of public oratory to America, viewing the multilevel political structure as ripe for "the renaissance of Demosthenic eloquence." Adams' <i>Lectures on Rhetoric and Oratory</i> (1810) looks at the fate of ancient oratory, the necessity of liberty for it to flourish, and its importance as a unifying element for a new nation of diverse cultures and beliefs. Just as civic eloquence failed to gain popularity in Britain, in the United States interest faded in the second decade of the 19th century as the "public spheres of heated oratory" disappeared in favor of the private sphere.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_U.S._minister_to_Russia">First U.S. minister to Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: First U.S. minister to Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> appointed Adams as the first ever <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Russia" title="United States Ambassador to Russia">United States Minister to Russia</a> in 1809 (though <a href="/wiki/Francis_Dana" title="Francis Dana">Francis Dana</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Short_(American_ambassador)" title="William Short (American ambassador)">William Short</a> had previously been nominated to the post, neither presented his credentials at <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>). After resigning his post at Harvard, Adams and his wife Louisa boarded a merchant ship in Boston on Aug. 5, 1809. Their youngest son was with them during the long and tedious voyage to St. Petersburg. Their voyage was temporarily interrupted outside the southern coast of Norway<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> due to the <a href="/wiki/Gunboat_War" title="Gunboat War">Gunboat War</a>. They were at first boarded by a British officer who examined their papers and then, later that day, by a <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegian</a> officer who ordered the ship to <a href="/wiki/Kristiansand" title="Kristiansand">Christiansand</a>. In Christiansand, Adams discovered thirty-eight U.S. vessels which had been detained by the <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegians</a> and decided to take whatever action necessary to gain the release of both ships and crew as soon as possible. <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> was under <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Danish</a> rule at the time, so the officer and pirates were actually <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegian</a>. The voyage to St. Petersburg resumed but was once again stopped by a British squadron. Adams showed his commission to <a href="/wiki/Sir_Albemarle_Bertie,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Albemarle Bertie, 1st Baronet">Admiral Albermarle Bertie</a>, the commander of the Squadron who recognized the usage of nations and Adams as an ambassador. The usage of nations is common laws of nations founded on custom. Because of the many delays, the Adams' did not arrive in St. Petersburg until October 23, 1809.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomatic_relations">Diplomatic relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Diplomatic relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Count <a href="/wiki/Nikolay_Rumyantsev" title="Nikolay Rumyantsev">Nikolay Rumyantsev</a>, Chancellor of the empire, formally received Adams, and requested a copy of his credential letter. Romanzoff assured Adams that his appointment pleased him personally. Adams presentation to the emperor was postponed however because of the temporary indisposition of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Alexander I</a>. Rumyantsev immediately invited Adams to a diplomatic dinner which included the French ambassador, <a href="/wiki/Armand_Augustin_Louis_de_Caulaincourt" title="Armand Augustin Louis de Caulaincourt" class="mw-redirect">Armand Augustin Louis de Caulaincourt</a>, Duke of Vicenza, numerous foreign ministers then at the Russian Court, and many of the nobility. This was the same mansion Adams had dined in 1781, as secretary of Francis Dana.</p>
<p>Tsar Alexander I received Adams alone in his cabinet where he expressed his pleasure at Adams' appointment. Adams told Alexander that "the President of the United States had desired him to express the hope that his mission would be considered as a proof of respect for the person and character of his majesty, as an acknowledgment of the many testimonies of good-will he had already given to the United States, and of a desire to strengthen commercial relations between them and his provinces." Alexander replied, that, "in everything depending on him, he should be happy to contribute to the increase of their friendly relations; that it was his wish to establish a just system of maritime rights, and that he should adhere invariably to those he had declared."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> After these official diplomatic greetings, Alexander and Adams discussed several other issues such as the policies of the different European powers, trade and commerce, and other mutually beneficial prospects, and that the Russian and U.S. could be very useful to each other.</p>
<p>Adams was also given private audiences with the empress and the dowager empress, who also received Louisa Adams. While not officially a diplomat, Louisa Adams did serve an invaluable role as wife-of-diplomat, becoming a favorite of the czar and making up for her husband's utter lack of charm. She was an indispensable part of the American mission.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Adams requested Rumyantsev to ask Alexander to act on behalf of the United States in securing the release of the American sailors and ships being held by the Danish. The Tsar ordered the Chancellor to request the release of the American property as soon as possible, which the Danish government complied with. Adams spent a great deal of time securing the release of American vessels and seamen from various "seizures and sequestrations."</p>
<p>In 1811, Adams received a commission from the Secretary of State as an Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States. Adams immediately declined and remained in St. Petersburg. In 1812, Adams reported the news of <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">Napoleon's invasion of Russia</a> and Napoleon's disastrous retreat. Also in 1812, Rumyantsev asked if he should request Alexander to mediate a pacification of hostilities between the United States and Great Britain. The U.S. accepted the offer and in July 1813, two associates of Adams, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_A._Bayard_(elder)" title="James A. Bayard (elder)">James A. Bayard</a> arrived in St. Petersburg to begin negotiations under mediation by Alexander. Gallatin was at that time Secretary of Treasury and the Senate rejected his appointment to the diplomatic mission as incompatible under the Constitution. However, this rejection did not occur until after Gallatin and Bayard had already left for St. Petersburg. In September, <a href="/wiki/Earl_Cathcart" title="Earl Cathcart">Lord William Cathcart</a> delivered a British memoir to Alexander explaining their reasons for declining the mediation. Thus ended President Madison's hope that Alexander could end the war.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Adams_in_Russian_society">Adams in Russian society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Adams in Russian society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Adams was well liked by the Russian Court and often would be met on walks by Alexander. The tsar asked Adams if he would be taking a house in the country over the summer. When Adams hesitated, the emperor stated with good humor that perhaps it was a financial consideration and Adams was able to respond in kind that it was in large part. Adams was a man who endeavored to live within the means provided by the American government.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Adams' were also provided with several invitations to various entertainments. "The formalities of these court presentations," Mr. Adams remarked, "are so trifling and insignificant in themselves, and so important in the eyes of princes and courtiers, that they are much more embarrassing to an American than business of greater importance. It is not safe or prudent to despise them, nor practicable for a person of rational understanding to value them."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams was concerned that the various balls and parties given by the Tsar and the foreign ministers, took too much time away from his official duties. Often these events would last until 4 a.m., so Adams stopped attending those he was able to avoid. The numerous diplomatic visits also annoyed Adams who wrote "I have been engaged the whole forenoon; and though I rise at six o'clock, I am sometimes unable to find time to write only part of a private letter in the course of the day. These visits take up so much of my time, that I sometimes think of taking a resolution not to receive them; but, on the other hand, so much information important to be possessed, and particularly relative to current political events, is to be collected from them, that they are rather to be encouraged than discountenanced."</p>
<p>In 1814, Adams was recalled from Russia to serve as chief negotiator of the U.S. commission for the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ghent" title="Treaty of Ghent">Treaty of Ghent</a>, which ended the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> between the United States and United Kingdom.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minister_to_the_court_of_St._James.27s">Minister to the court of St. James's</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Minister to the court of St. James's">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Finally, he was sent to be <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom">minister to the Court of St. James's</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a>) from 1815 until 1817, a post that was first held by his father.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-24"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The name is derived from its location at <a href="/wiki/St._James%27s_Palace" title="St. James's Palace" class="mw-redirect">St. James's Palace</a>. In London, Adams was part of a U.S. Legation consisting of himself, two young secretaries and a small office in Craven Street, London WC2.<sup id="cite_ref-sisters_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sisters-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Since they were not particularly well paid, Adams and his wife Louisa lived in <a href="/wiki/Ealing" title="Ealing">Ealing</a>, at that time a village in the countryside, in order to maintain the expensive carriages and liveries which social appearance demanded.<sup id="cite_ref-sisters_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sisters-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg/220px-1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg" width="220" height="319" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg/330px-1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg/440px-1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3201" data-file-height="4647" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1815 US passport issued by John Quincy Adams at London</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Secretary_of_State_.281817.E2.80.931825.29">U.S. Secretary of State (1817–1825)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: U.S. Secretary of State (1817–1825)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3883" data-file-height="4697" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Quincy Adams by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a>, 1818</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams served as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States" title="Cabinet of the United States">Cabinet</a> of President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> from 1817 until 1825. Typically, his views concurred with those espoused by Monroe. As Secretary of State, he negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a> (which acquired <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> for the United States), the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">Treaty of 1818</a>, and wrote the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>. Many historians regard him as one of the greatest Secretaries of State in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-Bemis_1949_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bemis_1949-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Herring.2C_George_p129_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herring.2C_George_p129-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Floridas, still a Spanish territory but with no Spanish presence to speak of, became a refuge for runaway slaves and native Americans. Monroe sent in General <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> who pushed the Seminole south, executed two British merchants who were supplying weapons, deposed one governor and named another, and left an American garrison in occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-accessmylibrary1_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-accessmylibrary1-37"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> President Monroe and all his cabinet, except Adams, believed Jackson had exceeded his instructions. Adams argued that since Spain had proved incapable of policing her territories, the United States was obliged to act in self-defense. Adams so ably justified Jackson's conduct that he silenced protests from either Spain or Britain; Congress refused to punish Jackson. Adams used the events that had unfolded in Florida to negotiate the Florida Treaty with Spain in 1819 that turned Florida over to the U.S. and resolved border issues regarding the Louisiana Purchase.<sup id="cite_ref-accessmylibrary1_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-accessmylibrary1-37"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Oregon_boundary_dispute" title="Oregon boundary dispute">Oregon boundary dispute</a>, Adams sought to negotiate a settlement with England to decide the border between the western United States and Canada. This would become the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">Treaty of 1818</a>. Along with the <a href="/wiki/Rush%E2%80%93Bagot_Treaty" title="Rush–Bagot Treaty">Rush–Bagot Treaty</a> of 1817, this marked the beginning of improved relations between the British Empire and its former colonies, and paved the way for better relations between the U.S. and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. The treaty had several provisions, but in particular it set the boundary between <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a> and the United States along the <a href="/wiki/49th_parallel_north" title="49th parallel north">49th parallel</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a>. This settled a boundary dispute caused by ignorance of actual geography in the boundary agreed to in the 1783 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris</a> that ended the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. That earlier treaty had used the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> to determine the border, but assumed that the river extended further north than it did, and so that earlier settlement was unworkable.</p>
<p>By the time Monroe became president, several European powers, in particular Spain, were attempting to re-establish control over South America.<sup id="cite_ref-Herring.2C_George_C._1776.2C_pp._153-155_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herring.2C_George_C._1776.2C_pp._153-155-38"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Independence Day</a> 1821, in response to those who advocated American support for independence movements in many South American countries,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams gave a speech in which he said that American policy was moral support for independence movements but not armed intervention. Adams foresaw what would befall the United States if it sacrificed its republican spirit on the altar of empire. He stated that America "goes not abroad in search of monsters to destroy" lest she "involve herself beyond power of extrication, in all wars of interest and intrigue, of individual avarice, envy, and ambition, which assume the colors and usurp the standard of freedom. The fundamental maxims of her policy would insensibly change from liberty to force." The United States, Adams warned, might "become the dictatress of the world [but] she would be no longer the ruler of her own spirit."<sup id="cite_ref-july4speech_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-july4speech-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> From this, Adams authored what came to be known as the Monroe Doctrine, which was introduced on December 2, 1823. It stated that further efforts by <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> countries to colonize land or interfere with states in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> would be viewed as acts of aggression requiring U.S. intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-res_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-res-41"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> The United States, reflecting concerns raised by Great Britain, ultimately hoped to avoid having any European power take over Spain's colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Herring.2C_George_C._1776.2C_pp._153-155_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herring.2C_George_C._1776.2C_pp._153-155-38"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> It became a defining moment in the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Foreign policy of the United States">foreign policy of the United States</a> and one of its longest-standing tenets, and would be invoked by many U.S. statesmen and several U.S. presidents, including <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and others.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1824_presidential_election">1824 presidential election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1824 presidential election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_1938_Issue-6c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/John_Quincy_Adams_1938_Issue-6c.jpg/150px-John_Quincy_Adams_1938_Issue-6c.jpg" width="150" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/John_Quincy_Adams_1938_Issue-6c.jpg/225px-John_Quincy_Adams_1938_Issue-6c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/John_Quincy_Adams_1938_Issue-6c.jpg/300px-John_Quincy_Adams_1938_Issue-6c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="543" data-file-height="624" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_1938_Issue-6c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/US_Presidents_on_US_postage_stamps" title="US Presidents on US postage stamps" class="mw-redirect">1938 stamp</a> of John Quincy Adams</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824" title="United States presidential election, 1824">1824 election</a> drew near people began looking for candidates. New England voters admired Adams' patriotism and political skills and it was mainly due to their support that he entered the race. The old caucus system of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> had collapsed; indeed the entire <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a> had collapsed and the election was a fight based on regional support. Adams had a strong base in New England. His opponents included <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, and the hero of New Orleans, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. During the campaign Calhoun dropped out, and Crawford fell ill giving further support to the other candidates. When Election Day arrived, Andrew Jackson won, although narrowly, pluralities of the popular and electoral votes, but not the necessary majority of electoral votes.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Quincy_Adams_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Quincy_Adams-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> With just over one-fourth voter turnout for the election, combined with Adams receiving less than one-third of the popular vote, Adams scored only 113,142 votes.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -55px; left: -45px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:ADAMS,_John_Q-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Adams as president" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="380" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6902" data-file-height="8066" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ADAMS,_John_Q-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:ADAMS, John Q-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:ADAMS, John Q-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Adams as president</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twelfth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twelfth Amendment</a>, the presidential election fell to the House of Representatives, which was to choose from the top three candidates: Jackson, Adams, and Crawford. Clay had come in fourth place and thus was not on the ballot, but he retained considerable power and influence as Speaker of the House.</p>
<p>Clay's personal dislike for Jackson and the similarity of his <a href="/wiki/American_System_(economic_plan)" title="American System (economic plan)">American System</a> to Adams' position on <a href="/wiki/Tariffs_in_United_States_history" title="Tariffs in United States history">tariffs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">internal improvements</a> caused him to throw his support to Adams, who was elected by the House on February 9, 1825, on the first ballot. Adams' victory shocked Jackson, who had won the most electoral and popular votes and fully expected to be elected president. When Adams appointed Clay as Secretary of State—the position that Adams and his three predecessors had held before becoming president—<a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian Democrats</a> were outraged, and claimed that Adams and Clay had struck a "<a href="/wiki/Corrupt_bargain" title="Corrupt bargain" class="mw-redirect">corrupt bargain</a>". This contention overshadowed Adams' term and greatly contributed to Adams' loss to Jackson four years later, in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">1828 election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Quincy_Adams_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Quincy_Adams-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281825.E2.80.931829.29">Presidency (1825–1829)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Presidency (1825–1829)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_P.A._Healy_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/George_P.A._Healy_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-George_P.A._Healy_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/George_P.A._Healy_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-George_P.A._Healy_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/George_P.A._Healy_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-George_P.A._Healy_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3876" data-file-height="5123" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_P.A._Healy_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Quincy Adams in a posthumous portrait created in 1858 by <a href="/wiki/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy" title="George Peter Alexander Healy">G.P.A. Healy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-metmuseum_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metmuseum-43"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams served as the sixth <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> from March 4, 1825, to March 4, 1829. He took the <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States#Use_of_Bibles" title="Oath of office of the President of the United States">oath of office</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_law" title="Constitutional law">book of constitutional law</a>, instead of the more traditional <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>. Adams proposed an elaborate program of <a href="/wiki/Internal_improvements" title="Internal improvements">internal improvements</a> (roads, ports and canals), a national university, and federal support for the arts and sciences. He favored a high tariff to encourage the building of factories, and restricted land sales to slow the movement west. Opposition from the states' rights faction of a hostile congress killed many of his proposals.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> He also reduced the <a href="/wiki/National_debt" title="National debt" class="mw-redirect">national debt</a> from $16 million to $5 million, the remainder of which was paid off by his immediate successor, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Paul Nagel argues that his political acumen was not any less developed than others were in his day, and notes that <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, one of the era's most astute politicians, was a principal adviser to Adams and supporter throughout his presidency. Nagel argues that Adams' political problems were the result of an unusually hostile Jacksonian faction, and Adams' own dislike of the office.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Although a product of the political culture of his day, he refused to play politics according to the usual rules and was not as aggressive in courting political support as he could have been. He was attacked by the followers of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Jackson</a>, who accused him of being a partner to a "<a href="/wiki/First_Corrupt_Bargain" title="First Corrupt Bargain" class="mw-redirect">corrupt bargain</a>" to obtain Clay's support in the election and then appoint him <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson defeated Adams in 1828, and created the modern <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Democratic party</a> thus inaugurating the <a href="/wiki/Second_Party_System" title="Second Party System">Second Party System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policies">Domestic policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Domestic policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_$1_Coin_obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/150px-John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg" width="150" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/225px-John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/300px-John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1098" data-file-height="1098" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_$1_Coin_obverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Presidential_Dollar" title="Presidential Dollar" class="mw-redirect">Presidential Dollar</a> of John Quincy Adams</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During his term, Adams worked on transforming America into a world power through "<a href="/wiki/Internal_improvements" title="Internal improvements">internal improvements</a>," as a part of the "<a href="/wiki/American_System_(economic_plan)" title="American System (economic plan)">American System</a>". It consisted of a high tariff to support internal improvements such as road-building, and a national bank to encourage productive enterprise and form a national currency. In his first annual message to Congress, Adams presented an ambitious program for modernization that included roads, canals, a national university,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> an astronomical observatory,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> and other initiatives. The support for his proposals was mixed, mainly due to opposition from Jackson's followers.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some of his proposals were adopted, specifically the extension of the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_Road" title="Cumberland Road" class="mw-redirect">Cumberland Road</a> into <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> with surveys for its continuation west to <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri" class="mw-redirect">St. Louis</a>; the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Ohio_Canal" title="Chesapeake and Ohio Canal">Chesapeake and Ohio Canal</a>, the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Delaware_Canal" title="Chesapeake and Delaware Canal">Chesapeake and Delaware Canal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Louisville_and_Portland_Canal" title="Louisville and Portland Canal">Louisville and Portland Canal</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Falls_of_the_Ohio" title="Falls of the Ohio" class="mw-redirect">falls of the Ohio</a>; the connection of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River system</a> in Ohio and Indiana; and the enlargement and rebuilding of the <a href="/wiki/Dismal_Swamp_Canal" title="Dismal Swamp Canal">Dismal Swamp Canal</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> One of the issues which divided the administration was protective tariffs, of which Henry Clay was a leading advocate. After Adams lost control of Congress in 1827, the situation became more complicated. By signing into law the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1828" title="Tariff of 1828" class="mw-redirect">Tariff of 1828</a>, quite unpopular in parts of the south, he further antagonized the Jacksonians.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Adams' generous policy toward Native Americans caused him trouble. Settlers on the frontier, who were constantly seeking to move westward, cried for a more expansionist policy. When the federal government tried to assert authority on behalf of the Cherokees, the governor of Georgia took up arms. Adams defended his domestic agenda as continuing Monroe's policies. In contrast, Andrew Jackson and Martin Van Buren instigated the policy of Indian removal to the west (i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policies">Foreign policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Foreign policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adams is regarded as one of the greatest diplomats in American history, and during his tenure as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, he was the chief designer of the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bemis_1956_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bemis_1956-53"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> He had witnessed the <a href="/wiki/First_Barbary_War" title="First Barbary War">First Barbary War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Barbary_War" title="Second Barbary War">Second Barbary War</a> against the Arab pirates of North Africa, and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a> from the Ottoman Turks. Public opinion in the U.S. strongly favored the Greek cause and such leaders as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> called for intervention. Adams strongly opposed any entanglement in European affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Charles Edel, Adams believed that, "Intervention would accomplish little, retard the cause of republicanism, and distract the country from its primary goal of continental expansion". Moreover, fearful that U.S. intentions would outstrip its capabilities, Adams thought that projecting U.S. power abroad would weaken its gravitational force on the North American continent.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On July 4, 1821, he gave an address to Congress:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>... But she [the United States of America] goes not abroad, in search of monsters to destroy. She is the well-wisher to the freedom and independence of all. She is the champion and vindicator only of her own.<sup id="cite_ref-july4speech_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-july4speech-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>During his term as president, however, Adams achieved little of long-term consequence in foreign affairs. A reason for this was the opposition he faced in Congress, where his rivals prevented him from succeeding.<sup id="cite_ref-Bemis_1956_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bemis_1956-53"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Among his diplomatic achievements were treaties of <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(international_relations)" title="Reciprocity (international relations)">reciprocity</a> with a number of nations, including <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> countries, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>. However, thanks to the successes of Adams' diplomacy during his previous eight years as secretary of state, most of the foreign policy issues he would have faced had been resolved by the time he became president.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_cabinet">Administration and cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Administration and cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
