<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointments">Appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_John_Quincy_Adams" title="List of federal judges appointed by John Quincy Adams">List of federal judges appointed by John Quincy Adams</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supreme_Court">Supreme Court</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Supreme Court">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Trimble" title="Robert Trimble">Robert Trimble</a> – June 16, 1826 – August 25, 1828</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: States admitted to the Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>None</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Departure">Departure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Departure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>John Quincy Adams left office on March 4, 1829, after losing the election of 1828 to <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. Adams did not attend the inauguration of his successor, Andrew Jackson, who had openly snubbed him by refusing to pay the traditional "courtesy call" to the outgoing president during the weeks before his own inauguration.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> He was one of only four presidents who chose not to attend their respective successor's inauguration; the others were <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">his father</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>, and Richard Nixon.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1828_presidential_election">1828 presidential election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 1828 presidential election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">United States presidential election, 1828</a></div>
<p>After the inauguration of Adams in 1825, Jackson resigned from his senate seat. For four years he worked hard, with help from his supporters in Congress, to defeat Adams in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">presidential election of 1828</a>. The campaign was very much a personal one. As was the tradition of the day and age in American presidential politics, neither candidate personally campaigned, but their political followers organized many campaign events. Both candidates were rhetorically attacked in the press. This reached a low point when the press accused Jackson's wife <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Jackson" title="Rachel Jackson">Rachel</a> of bigamy. She died a few weeks after the elections. Jackson said he would forgive those who insulted him, but he would never forgive the ones who had attacked his wife.</p>
<p>Adams lost the election by a decisive margin. He won all the same states that his father had won in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">election of 1800</a>: the New England states, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, as well as parts of New York and a majority of Maryland. Jackson won the rest of the states, picking up 178 electoral votes to Adams' 83 votes, and succeeded him. Adams and his father were the only U.S. presidents to serve a single term during the first 48 years of the Presidency (1789–1837). Historian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Bailey" title="Thomas A. Bailey">Thomas Bailey</a> observed, "Seldom has the public mind been so successfully poisoned against an honest and high-minded man."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_congressional_career_.281830.E2.80.931848.29">Later congressional career (1830–1848)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Later congressional career (1830–1848)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Adams did not retire after leaving office. Instead he ran for and won a seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1830" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1830">1830 elections</a>. This went against the generally held opinion that former Presidents should not run for public office. He was the first President to serve in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> after his term of office, and one of only two former presidents to do so (<a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a> later served in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>). He was elected to nine terms, serving as a Representative for 17 years, from 1831 until his death.</p>
<p>Adams ran for Governor of Massachusetts in 1833 on the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Masonic_Party" title="Anti-Masonic Party">Anti-Masonic</a> ticket.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Incumbent <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republican</a> Governor <a href="/w/index.php?title=Levi_Lincoln_Jr.&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Levi Lincoln Jr. (page does not exist)">Levi Lincoln Jr.</a> was retiring and Adams faced National Republican <a href="/wiki/John_Davis_(Massachusetts_governor)" title="John Davis (Massachusetts governor)">John Davis</a>, Democrat <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Morton" title="Marcus Morton">Marcus Morton</a> and Samuel L. Allen of the <a href="/wiki/Working_Men%27s_Party" title="Working Men's Party">Working Men's Party</a>. Davis won a plurality, 40%, and Adams took 29% with Morton taking 25% and Allen 6%. Because no candidate had won a majority, the election was sent to the state legislature to decide. Adams withdrew and endorsed Davis, preferring him over Morton, and Davis was chosen by the legislature in January 1834.</p>
<p>In authoring a change to the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1828" title="Tariff of 1828" class="mw-redirect">Tariff of 1828</a>, he was instrumental to the compromise that ended the <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a>. When <a href="/wiki/James_Smithson" title="James Smithson">James Smithson</a> died and left his estate to the U.S. government to build an institution of learning, many in Congress wanted to use the money for other purposes. Adams was key to ensuring that the money was instead used to build the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Committee_assignments">Committee assignments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Committee assignments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Congress, he was chair of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Energy_and_Commerce" title="United States House Committee on Energy and Commerce">Committee on Commerce and Manufactures</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Natural_Resources" title="United States House Committee on Natural Resources">Committee on Indian Affairs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Foreign_Affairs" title="United States House Committee on Foreign Affairs">Committee on Foreign Affairs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Congressional_biography_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Congressional_biography-59"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams_and_abolitionism" title="John Quincy Adams and abolitionism">John Quincy Adams and abolitionism</a></div>
<p>A longtime opponent of slavery, Adams used his new role in Congress to fight it. In 1836, Southern Representatives voted in a "<a href="/wiki/Gag_rule" title="Gag rule">gag rule</a>" that immediately tabled any petitions about slavery, thus preventing any discussion or debate of the slavery issue. He became a forceful opponent of this rule and conceived a way around it, attacking slavery in the House for two weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The gag rule prevented him from bringing slavery petitions to the floor, but he brought one anyway. It was a petition from a Georgia citizen urging disunion due to the continuation of slavery in the South. Though he certainly did not support it and made that clear at the time, his intent was to antagonize the pro-slavery faction of Congress into an open fight on the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The plan worked.</p>
<p>The petition infuriated his Congressional enemies, many of whom were agitating for disunion themselves. They moved for his censure over the matter, enabling Adams to discuss slavery openly during his subsequent defense. Taking advantage of his right to defend himself, Adams delivered prepared and impromptu remarks against slavery and in favor of abolition.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Knowing that he would probably be acquitted, he changed the focus from his own actions to those of the slaveholders, speaking against the slave trade and the ownership of slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Art.26Archives_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Art.26Archives-60"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> He decided that if he were censured, he would merely resign, run for the office again, and probably win easily.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> When his opponents realized that they played into his political strategy, they tried to bury the censure. Adams made sure this did not happen, and the debate continued. He attacked slavery and slaveholders as immoral and condemned the institution while calling for it to end.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> After two weeks, a vote was held, and he was not censured. He delighted in the misery he was inflicting on the slaveholders he so hated, and prided himself on being "obnoxious to the slave faction."<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although the censure of Adams over the slavery petition was ultimately abandoned, the House did address the issue of petitions from enslaved persons at a later time. Adams again argued that the right to petition was a universal right, granted by God, so that those in the weakest positions might always have recourse to those in the most powerful. Adams also called into question the actions of a House that would limit its own ability to debate and resolve questions internally. After this debate, the gag rule was ultimately retained.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-61"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The discussion ignited by his actions and the attempts of others to quiet him raised questions of the right to petition, the right to legislative debate, and the morality of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> During the censure debate, Adams said that he took delight in the fact that southerners would forever remember him as "the acutest, the astutest, the archest enemy of southern slavery that ever existed".<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1844, he chaired a committee for reform of the rules of Congress, and he used this opportunity to try once again to repeal the gag rule. He spent two months building support for this move, but due to northern opposition, the rule narrowly survived.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> He fiercely criticized northern Representatives and Senators, in particular <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a>, who seemed to cater to the slave faction in exchange for southern support.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> His opposition to slavery made him, along with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, one of the leading opponents of Texas annexation and the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a>. He correctly predicted that both would contribute to <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">civil war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> After one of his reelection victories, he said that he must "bring about a day prophesied when slavery and war shall be banished from the face of the earth."<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> He wrote in his private journal in 1820:<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The discussion of this <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri question</a> has betrayed the secret of their souls. In the abstract they admit that slavery is an evil, they disclaim it, and cast it all upon the shoulder of…Great Britain. But when probed to the quick upon it, they show at the bottom of their souls pride and vainglory in their condition of masterdom. They look down upon the simplicity of a Yankee’s manners, because he has no habits of overbearing like theirs and cannot treat negroes like dogs. It is among the evils of slavery that it taints the very sources of moral principle. It establishes false estimates of virtue and vice: for what can be more false and heartless than this doctrine which makes the first and holiest rights of humanity to depend upon the color of the skin?</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1841, at the request of <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Tappan" title="Lewis Tappan">Lewis Tappan</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ellis_Gray&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ellis Gray (page does not exist)">Ellis Gray</a>, Adams joined the case of <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._The_Amistad" title="United States v. The Amistad">United States v. The Amistad</a></i>. Adams went before the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> on behalf of African slaves who had revolted and seized the Spanish ship <i>Amistad</i>. Adams appeared on 24 February 1841, and spoke for four hours. His argument succeeded; the Court ruled in favor of the Africans, who were declared free and returned to their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photography">Photography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Photography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Q._Adams.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/John_Q._Adams.jpg/170px-John_Q._Adams.jpg" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/John_Q._Adams.jpg/255px-John_Q._Adams.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/John_Q._Adams.jpg/340px-John_Q._Adams.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2838" data-file-height="3580" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Q._Adams.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Collodion_process" title="Collodion process">Glass collodion negative copy</a> c. 1860 of a <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a> of John Quincy Adams in 1847 or 1848, attributed to <a href="/wiki/Mathew_Brady" title="Mathew Brady">Mathew Brady</a> (retouched)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1843, Adams sat for the earliest confirmed photograph still in existence of a U.S. president, although other sources contend that <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> had posed even earlier for his portrait, in 1841.<sup id="cite_ref-whha-16_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whha-16-64"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> The original <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a> is in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">National Portrait Gallery</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-npg-search_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npg-search-65"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Living_Bridge_between_American_Revolution_and_Civil_War">Living Bridge between American Revolution and Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Living Bridge between American Revolution and Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although there is no indication that the two were close, Adams met <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> during the latter's sole term as a member of the House of Representatives, from 1847 until Adams' death.<sup id="cite_ref-unger_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unger-66"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Thus, it has been suggested that Adams is the only major figure in American history who knew both the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers</a> and Abraham Lincoln, though Martin Van Buren met Founding Fathers <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, knew Founder <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> (Van Buren's mentor), and met the young Lincoln while on a campaign trip through Illinois.<sup id="cite_ref-widmer_ted_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-widmer_ted-67"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nullification_crisis">Nullification crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Nullification crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Besides his opposition to slavery and the <a href="/wiki/Gag_rule" title="Gag rule">gag rule</a> (discussed above), his congressional career is remembered for several other key accomplishments. Shortly after Adams entered Congress, the <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a> threatened civil war over the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1828" title="Tariff of 1828" class="mw-redirect">Tariff of 1828</a>. Adams authored an alteration to the tariff, which weakened it and diffused the crisis. Congress also passed the <a href="/wiki/Force_Bill" title="Force Bill">Force Bill</a> which authorized President <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> to use military force if Adams' compromise bill did not force the belligerent states to capitulate. There was no need, however, because Adams' compromise defused the issue. The compromise actually did not alter the tariff as much as the southern states had hoped, though they agreed not to continue pursuing the issue for fear of civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_(detail),_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG/170px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG/255px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG/340px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3240" data-file-height="4320" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_(detail),_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Detail view of the portrait of <i>Adams</i> in the <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">U.S. National Portrait Gallery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, painted by <a href="/wiki/George_Caleb_Bingham" title="George Caleb Bingham">George Caleb Bingham</a>; this copy c. 1850 from an original of 1844</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advancement_of_science">Advancement of science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Advancement of science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adams also became a leading force for the advancement of science. As president, he had proposed a national observatory, which did not win much support. In 1829 British scientist <a href="/wiki/James_Smithson" title="James Smithson">James Smithson</a> died, and left his fortune for the "increase and diffusion of knowledge." In Smithson's will, he stated that should his nephew, Henry James Hungerford, die without heirs, the Smithson estate would go to the government of the United States to create an "Establishment for the increase &amp; diffusion of Knowledge among men." After the nephew died without heirs in 1835, President <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> informed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> of the bequest, which amounted to about <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>500,000 ($75,000,000 in 2008 U.S. dollars after inflation). Adams realized that this might allow the United States to realize his dream of building a national institution of science and learning. Adams thus became Congress' primary supporter of what would become the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>. He also relentlessly pursued support for astronomical efforts and observatories, seeking a national observatory for the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-portolano_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-portolano-69"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> His efforts eventually led to what is now the United States' oldest, still-operational scientific institution, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory" title="United States Naval Observatory">United States Naval Observatory</a>. In 1825 Adams signed a bill for the creation of a national observatory just before leaving presidential office – which became the Naval Observatory. Adams in fact spent many nights at the Observatory, with celebrated national astronomer and oceanographer <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Fontaine_Maury" title="Matthew Fontaine Maury">Matthew Fontaine Maury</a>, watching and charting the stars, which had always been one of Adams' avocations.</p>
<p>As for efforts to found the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>, the money was invested in shaky state bonds, which quickly defaulted. After heated debate in Congress, Adams successfully argued to restore the lost funds with interest.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Though Congress wanted to use the money for other purposes, Adams successfully persuaded Congress to preserve the money for an institution of science and learning.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress also debated whether the federal government had the authority to accept the gift, though with Adams leading the initiative, Congress decided to accept the legacy bequeathed to the nation and pledged the faith of the United States to the charitable trust on July 1, 1836.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG/220px-JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG/330px-JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG/440px-JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3901" data-file-height="2925" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Adams's <a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">cenotaph</a> at the Congressional Cemetery</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1846, the 78-year-old former president suffered a stroke that left him partially paralyzed. After a few months of rest, he made a full recovery and resumed his duties in Congress. When Adams entered the House chamber, everyone "stood up and applauded."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> On February 21, 1848, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> was discussing the matter of honoring U.S. Army officers who served in the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a>. Adams had been a vehement critic of the war, and as Congressmen rose up to say, "Aye!" in favor of the measure, he instead yelled, "No!"<sup id="cite_ref-parker-1848_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parker-1848-73"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> He rose to answer a question put forth by the Speaker of the House.<sup id="cite_ref-donaldson_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donaldson-74"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Immediately thereafter, Adams collapsed, having suffered a massive <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemorrhage" title="Cerebral hemorrhage">cerebral hemorrhage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-widmer-2008_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-widmer-2008-75"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> Two days later, on February 23, he died with his wife and youngest son at his side in the Speaker's Room inside the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">Capitol Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> His last words were "This is the last of earth. I am content." He died at 7:20&#160;p.m.<sup id="cite_ref-donaldson_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donaldson-74"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>His original interment was temporary, in the public vault at the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Cemetery" title="Congressional Cemetery">Congressional Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> Later, he was interred in the family burial ground in Quincy, Massachusetts, across from the First Parish Church, called <a href="/wiki/Hancock_Cemetery" title="Hancock Cemetery">Hancock Cemetery</a>. After Louisa's death in 1852, his son <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams Sr." class="mw-redirect">Charles Francis Adams</a> had his parents reinterred in the expanded family crypt in the <a href="/wiki/United_First_Parish_Church,_Quincy,_Massachusetts" title="United First Parish Church, Quincy, Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect">United First Parish Church</a> across the street, next to John and Abigail. Both tombs are viewable by the public. Adams' original tomb at Hancock Cemetery is still there and marked simply "J.Q. Adams".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>John Quincy Adams and <a href="/wiki/Louisa_Catherine_Adams" title="Louisa Catherine Adams" class="mw-redirect">Louisa Catherine Adams</a> had three sons and a daughter. Their daughter, Louisa, was born in 1811 but died in 1812 while the family was in Russia. They named their first son <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Adams" title="George Washington Adams">George Washington Adams</a> (1801–1829) after the first president. Both George and their second son, <a href="/wiki/John_Adams_II" title="John Adams II">John</a> (1803–1834), led troubled lives and died in early adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> (George committed suicide and John was expelled from Harvard before his 1823 graduation.)</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JQ_Adams_Vault,_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/82/JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg/220px-JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/82/JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg/330px-JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/82/JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg/440px-JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JQ_Adams_Vault,_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Quincy Adams' original tomb at <a href="/wiki/Hancock_Cemetery" title="Hancock Cemetery">Hancock Cemetery</a>, across the street from United First Parish Church</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams' youngest son, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams Sr." class="mw-redirect">Charles Francis Adams</a> (who named his own son <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams_II" title="John Quincy Adams II">John Quincy</a>), also pursued a career in diplomacy and politics. In 1870 Charles Francis built the first memorial <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">presidential library</a> in the United States, to honor his father. The Stone Library includes over 14,000 books written in twelve languages. The library is located in the "<a href="/wiki/Peacefield" title="Peacefield">Old House</a>" at <a href="/wiki/Adams_National_Historical_Park" title="Adams National Historical Park">Adams National Historical Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Massachusetts" title="Quincy, Massachusetts">Quincy, Massachusetts</a>.</p>
<p>John Adams and John Quincy Adams were the only father and son to serve as presidents until <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> (1989–1993) and <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> (2001–2009).</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>Adams has been determined to have the highest I.Q. of any U.S. President.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_the_Adams,_Quincy,_Massachusetts.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/220px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/330px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/440px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_the_Adams,_Quincy,_Massachusetts.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tombs of Presidents <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> (left) and John Quincy Adams (right) and their wives, in a family crypt beneath the <a href="/wiki/United_First_Parish_Church,_Quincy,_Massachusetts" title="United First Parish Church, Quincy, Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect">United First Parish Church</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams_Birthplace" title="John Quincy Adams Birthplace">John Quincy Adams Birthplace</a> is now part of <a href="/wiki/Adams_National_Historical_Park" title="Adams National Historical Park">Adams National Historical Park</a> and open to the public. The name Quincy has been used for at least nineteen other places in the United States. Those places were either directly or indirectly named for John Quincy Adams (for example, <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Illinois" title="Quincy, Illinois">Quincy, Illinois</a>, was named in honor of Adams while <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_California" title="Quincy, California">Quincy, California</a>, was named for Quincy, Illinois).</p>
<p>Adams was the first president to have his photograph taken.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> He also became the first president to adopt a short haircut instead of long hair tied in a queue and to regularly wear long <a href="/wiki/Trousers" title="Trousers">trousers</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Culottes" title="Culottes">knee breeches</a>. He is probably best known as a diplomat who shaped America's foreign policy in accordance with his ardently nationalist views, and is widely considered by historians to have been one of the greatest diplomats in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-Bemis_1949_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bemis_1949-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> He was key to the negotiation of several important treaties, such as the Treaty of Ghent, which ended the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, and the Florida Treaty, which resulted in the annexation of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. He also formulated the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>, which is still evoked to the present day. He is viewed by many as the exemplar and moral leader in an era of modernization. During this era, new technologies and networks of infrastructure and communication brought to the people messages of religious revival, social reform, and party politics, as well as moving goods, money, and people ever more rapidly and efficiently.<sup id="cite_ref-See_Howe_2007_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-See_Howe_2007-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Though he was always quite hostile to slavery, nearly to be point of being an <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionist</a> (although he doubted the abolitionists could successfully end slavery), he grew even more hostile to it later in life.<sup id="cite_ref-Lynn_H._Parsons_1999_p._232_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lynn_H._Parsons_1999_p._232-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams became a leading opponent of slave power and articulated a theory whereby the president could abolish slavery by using his <a href="/wiki/War_Powers_Clause" title="War Powers Clause">war powers</a>, a correct prediction of <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>'s use of the <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> in 1863. Adams predicted the likelihood of the Union's dissolution over the slavery issue, and was a key opponent of the Mexican–American War for this reason.<sup id="cite_ref-Parsons.2C_1999_p._162_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parsons.2C_1999_p._162-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Though he later described his presidency as the unhappiest time of his life,<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> scholars rate John Quincy Adams in the second quartile in the majority of <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">historical presidential rankings</a>.</p>
<p>Historians have often included Adams among the leading conservatives of his day.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mattie_2003_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mattie_2003-82"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Russell_Kirk" title="Russell Kirk">Russell Kirk</a>, however, sees Adams as a flawed conservative who was imprudent in opposing slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Mattie_2003_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mattie_2003-82"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diaries">Diaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Diaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of Adams' most important legacies is his massive diary,<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> which he began at age 11 with the simple entry "A journal, by me, J.Q.A." The Diary, housed at the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Historical_Society#Collections" title="Massachusetts Historical Society">Massachusetts Historical Society</a>, covers, in extraordinary detail, Adams' life and experiences up to his death in 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-Masshist.org_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masshist.org-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The massive fifty volumes are one of the most extensive collections of first-hand information from that period of the early American republic, and are cited by historians in a wide range of matters pertaining to that period.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Personality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adams' personality was much like that of his father, as were his political beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Throughout his life, he always preferred reading in seclusion to attending social engagements, and several times had to be pressured by others to remain in public service. Historian Paul Nagel argues that, like <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> after him, Adams suffered from depression for much of his life. Early in his life he sought some form of treatment. Adams thought his depression was due to the high expectations demanded of him by his father and mother. Throughout his life he felt inadequate and socially awkward because of his depression, and was constantly bothered by his physical appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> He was closer to his father, whom he spent much of his early life with abroad, than he was to his mother. When he was younger and the American Revolution was going on, his mother told her children what their father was doing, and what he was risking, and because of this Adams grew to greatly respect his father.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> His relationship with his mother was rocky; she had high expectations of him and was afraid her children might end up a dead alcoholic like her brother.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> As Abigail Adams had feared, John Quincy's brother, Charles, would eventually follow this fate.</p>
<p>John Quincy fell in love shortly after he finished school, but his mother did not approve of his considering marriage when he was still dependent on his parents for support, and the relationship ended. When he fell in love with his future wife, <a href="/wiki/Louisa_Johnson" title="Louisa Johnson" class="mw-redirect">Louisa Johnson</a>, his mother disapproved of this relationship as well. His biographer, Nagel, concludes that this disapproval motivated him to marry Johnson in 1797, despite Adams' reservations that Johnson, like his mother, had a strong a personality.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Marquis de Lafayette" class="mw-redirect">Marquis de Lafayette</a> once gave Adams an alligator as a gift, which he lodged for months in the unfinished East Room of the White House, before building it its own lodge. Reports indicate he enjoyed showing it to visitors.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antislavery_advocacy">Antislavery advocacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Antislavery advocacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JQAdams_house_TremontSt_BoylstonSt_Boston.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/JQAdams_house_TremontSt_BoylstonSt_Boston.png/170px-JQAdams_house_TremontSt_BoylstonSt_Boston.png" width="170" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/JQAdams_house_TremontSt_BoylstonSt_Boston.png/255px-JQAdams_house_TremontSt_BoylstonSt_Boston.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/JQAdams_house_TremontSt_BoylstonSt_Boston.png/340px-JQAdams_house_TremontSt_BoylstonSt_Boston.png 2x" data-file-width="449" data-file-height="511" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JQAdams_house_TremontSt_BoylstonSt_Boston.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Adams lived at the corner of <a href="/wiki/Tremont_Street" title="Tremont Street">Tremont</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boylston_Street" title="Boylston Street">Boylston Streets</a>, Boston, 1806–1809 <sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before 1820, Adams was best known as an exponent of American nationalism. Later in life, especially after his election to the House, he was famous as the most prominent national leader opposing slavery. He was not an abolitionist, say biographers Nagel and Parsons.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_8-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Remini notes that Adams thought the end of slavery would come by either civil war or the consent of the slave South, but definitely not through the work of abolitionists.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The turning point came with the debate on the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> in 1820 when he broke with his friend <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, who became the most outspoken national leader in favor of slavery. They became bitter enemies. Adams vilified slavery as a terrible evil and preached total abolition, while Calhoun countered that the right to own slaves had to be protected from interference from the federal government to keep the nation alive. Adams said slavery contradicted the principles of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republicanism</a>, while Calhoun said that slavery was essential to American democracy, for it made all white men equal. Both men pulled away from nationalism, and started to consider dissolution of the Union as a way of resolving the slavery predicament. Adams predicted that if the South formed a new nation, it would be torn apart by an extremely violent slave insurrection. If the two nations went to war, Adams predicted the president of the United States would use his war powers to abolish slavery. The two men became ideological leaders of the North and the South.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> In the House Adams became a champion of free speech, demanding that petitions against slavery be heard despite a "gag rule" that said they could not be heard.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_drawing2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/John_Quincy_Adams_drawing2.jpg/220px-John_Quincy_Adams_drawing2.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/John_Quincy_Adams_drawing2.jpg/330px-John_Quincy_Adams_drawing2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/John_Quincy_Adams_drawing2.jpg/440px-John_Quincy_Adams_drawing2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3945" data-file-height="3236" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_drawing2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Quincy Adams during his final hours of life after his collapse in the Capitol. Drawing in pencil by Arthur Joseph Stansbury, digitally restored.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1841, Adams had the case of a lifetime, representing the defendants in <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._The_Amistad" title="United States v. The Amistad">United States v. The Amistad Africans</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>. He successfully argued that the Africans, who had seized control of a Spanish ship on which they were being transported illegally as slaves, should not be extradited or deported to Cuba (a Spanish colony where slavery was legal) but should be considered free. Under President <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, the government argued the Africans should be deported for having mutinied and killed officers on the ship. Adams won their freedom, with the chance to stay in the United States or return to Africa. Adams made the argument because the U.S. had prohibited the international slave trade, although it allowed internal slavery. He never billed for his services in the <i>Amistad</i> case.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> The speech was directed not only at the justices of this Supreme Court hearing the case, but also to the broad national audience he instructed in the evils of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Adams repeatedly spoke out against the "<a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a>", that is the organized political power of the slave owners who dominated all the southern states and their representation in Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> He vehemently attacked the annexation of Texas (1845) and the Mexican War (1846–48) as part of a "conspiracy" to extend slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_television">Film and television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Film and television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adams occasionally is featured in the mass media. In the <a href="/wiki/Public_Broadcasting_Service" title="Public Broadcasting Service" class="mw-redirect">PBS</a> miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adams_Chronicles" title="The Adams Chronicles">The Adams Chronicles</a></i> (1976), he was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/David_Birney" title="David Birney">David Birney</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Daniels" title="William Daniels">William Daniels</a>, Marcel Trenchard, Steven Grover and Mark Winkworth. He was also portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Hopkins" title="Anthony Hopkins">Anthony Hopkins</a> in the 1997 film <i><a href="/wiki/Amistad_(film)" title="Amistad (film)">Amistad</a></i>, and again by <a href="/wiki/Ebon_Moss-Bachrach" title="Ebon Moss-Bachrach">Ebon Moss-Bachrach</a> and Steven Hinkle in the 2008 <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> television miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/John_Adams_(TV_miniseries)" title="John Adams (TV miniseries)" class="mw-redirect">John Adams</a></i>; the HBO series received criticism for needless historical and temporal distortions in its portrayal.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronunciation_note">Pronunciation note</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Pronunciation note">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Quincy family name was pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/?/ short 'i' in 'bid'">?</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="'z' in 'Zion'">z</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>, as is the name of the city in Massachusetts where Adams was born. All of the other Quincy place names are locally <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/?/ short 'i' in 'bid'">?</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>. Though not accurate, this pronunciation is also commonly used for Adams' middle name.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

"John Quincy Adams's Rhetorical Crusade for Astronomy". <i>Isis</i> <b>91</b> (3): 480–503. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1086%2F384852">10.1086/384852</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0021-1753">0021-1753</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier" class="mw-redirect">PMID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11143785">11143785</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Quincy+Adams&amp;rft.atitle=John+Quincy+Adams%27s+Rhetorical+Crusade+for+Astronomy&amp;rft.aufirst=Marlana&amp;rft.aulast=Portolano&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1086%2F384852&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F11143785&amp;rft.issn=0021-1753&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Isis&amp;rft.pages=480-503&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=91" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> Fulltext online at Jstor and Ebsco. He tried to create a national observatory, which became the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory" title="United States Naval Observatory">U.S. Naval Observatory</a>.</li>
<li><cite class="citation journal">Potkay, Adam S. (1999). "Theorizing Civic Eloquence in the Early Republic: the Road from David Hume to John Quincy Adams". <i>Early American Literature</i> <b>34</b> (2): 147–170. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0012-8163">0012-8163</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Quincy+Adams&amp;rft.atitle=Theorizing+Civic+Eloquence+in+the+Early+Republic%3A+the+Road+from+David+Hume+to+John+Quincy+Adams&amp;rft.aufirst=Adam+S.&amp;rft.aulast=Potkay&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issn=0012-8163&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=Early+American+Literature&amp;rft.pages=147-170&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=34" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> Fulltext online at Swetswise and Ebsco.</li>
<li><cite class="citation journal">Rathbun, Lyon (2000). "The Ciceronian Rhetoric of John Quincy Adams". <i>Rhetorica</i> <b>18</b> (2): 175–215. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1525%2Frh.2000.18.2.175">10.1525/rh.2000.18.2.175</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0734-8584">0734-8584</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Quincy+Adams&amp;rft.atitle=The+Ciceronian+Rhetoric+of+John+Quincy+Adams&amp;rft.aufirst=Lyon&amp;rft.aulast=Rathbun&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1525%2Frh.2000.18.2.175&amp;rft.issn=0734-8584&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=Rhetorica&amp;rft.pages=175-215&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=18" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>

New York: Times Books. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8050-6939-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-8050-6939-9">0-8050-6939-9</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJohn+Quincy+Adams&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+V.&amp;rft.aulast=Remini&amp;rft.btitle=John+Quincy+Adams&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-8050-6939-9&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Times+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>
